Evidence and Gender Determination in Marriage Claims Involving a Khuntha
Ruling on evidence in marriage claim involving a deceased khuntha

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on evidence precedence when a woman claims marriage to a deceased khuntha, distinguishing between the khuntha's life and death.

Core Idea
If a khuntha dies young, a woman's claim with evidence of male urination is accepted over rival evidence; if alive, judgment is suspended until maturity.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq deals with a complex scenario: a woman claims she was married to a khuntha who died young. The key legal question is how to evaluate conflicting evidence about the khuntha's gender and the marriage itself. You will learn the Hanafi rules for priority of evidence and why the status of the khuntha (alive or dead) fundamentally changes the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario
A woman brings evidence that a deceased khuntha was married to her and that he was male. The khuntha's brother denies this and may bring counter-evidence that the khuntha was female. The judge must decide based on the evidence's timing and content.
The khuntha died young without clear gender signs.
2
Analyze the first ruling: simultaneous evidence
If both sides bring evidence together (woman & brother), the judge prefers the woman's evidence because she is the one claiming the marriage, and her evidence also establishes the khuntha as male (which is necessary for a valid marriage). The brother's evidence about femaleness is not sufficient to overturn her claim.
The woman's evidence serves two purposes: proving marriage and proving male gender.
3
Analyze the second ruling: time-stamped evidence
If one party presents evidence earlier, that evidence is accepted if the later evidence is not stronger. But if both are undated, they cancel each other. If one is dated earlier, it prevails.
Precedence by date is a general principle in evidence law.
4
Understand the life/death distinction
If the khuntha is still alive, all these rulings are suspended. The judge waits until the khuntha reaches maturity and natural signs appear (i.e., urination pattern or other signs of puberty). During life, a definitive judgment cannot be made because the khuntha's status is still ambiguous.
This shows the Hanafi preference for certainty over speculation.
5
Apply the precedent rule
If a judgment is already passed based on one party's evidence, later contrary evidence is not accepted. The earlier judgment is final unless it was clearly flawed.
This mirrors the general principle of res judicata.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أن هذا الخنثى المشكل الذي مات صغيراً وترك مالاً أقامت امرأة بينة أن أباه زوجها إياه في حياته ومهرها ألف درهم وأنه كان غلاماً يبول من حيث يبول الغلام ولم يكن يبول من حيث يبول النساء وكذبها الأخ ابن الميت قال : أصدق المرأة واجعله غلاماً واجعل صداقها في ميراثه من ميراث الغلام، فإن أقام الأخ ابن الميت البينة بأنه كان جارية يبول من حيث تبول الجارية قال : لا أقبل بينتهما على ذلك فأقضي ببينة المرأة. تدعي وهذا إذا جاءا معاً، فأما إذا أقام الزوج البيئة أولاً وقضى القاضي بذلك ثم أقامت المرأة البينة فإنه لا يقبل منها لترجح الأولى بالقضاء، فإن وقت أحد من البيتين وقتاً قبل الأخرى فإنه يقضى بأسبقهما تاريخاً وإن لم يوقت ذكر أنهما يبطلان. وهذا إذا كانت المرأة الصداق ، ل، ومتى لم تدع فإنها تتهاتر البيئتان وإن كان هذا حياً لم يمت والمسألة بحالها قال : هذا كله باطل ولا أقضي بشيء منه بلا توقف في ذلك حتى يستبين حاله متی أدرك ، وليس حالة الحياة عندي بمنزلة ما بعد الموت. ولو أن هذا الخنثى حين مات بعد أبيه وهو مراهق أقام رجل البينة أن أباه زوجه إياها على هذا الوقف بألف وأمره بدفعه إليه وأنه كان يبول من حيث تبول النساء وكذبه الورثة فهذا على وجهين أما إن جاءت البينات معاً أو جاءت إحداهما قبل صاحبتها، فإن جاءتا معاً فلا يخلو إما إن لم يوقتا أو وقتا وقتاً ووقتهما على السواء أو كان وقت أحدهما أسبق، فإن لم يوقتا أو وقتا ووقتهما على السواء تهاترت البينات جميعاً، وهذا بخلاف ما لو لم يدع الزوج نصف الطلاق بالطلاق قبل الدخول. وإنما ادعى النكاح على الخنثى لا غير وباقي المسألة بحالها ذكر أن البينة المثبتة أنها امرأة وأولى. وإن وقتا وقتاً ووقت أحدهما أسبق فالسابق أولى، فإن جاءت إحداهما قبل الأخرى إن جاءت
And if this ambiguous khuntha died young and left property, and a woman brought evidence that his father married him (the khuntha) to her during his lifetime with a dower of one thousand dirhams, and that he was a boy who urinated from where boys urinate and did not urinate from where women urinate, and the brother, son of the deceased, denied her. He said: I affirm the woman and consider him a boy, and place her dower in his inheritance from the inheritance of a boy. If the brother, son of the deceased, brings evidence that he was a girl who urinates from where girls urinate, he said: I do not accept both their evidences, so I give judgment by the woman's evidence. She claims, and this is if they come together. As for if the husband brings evidence first and the judge gives judgment accordingly, then the woman brings evidence, it is not accepted from her because the first is strengthened by the judgment. If one of the two parties times (dates) their evidence before the other, judgment is given for the one earlier in date. If they do not time, it is mentioned that both are void. And this is if the woman [claims] the dower? And if she does not claim, then the two evidences cancel each other. And if this (khuntha) is alive and has not died, and the case is as is, he said: All this is invalid, and I do not give judgment on anything from it without delay until his situation becomes clear when he reaches maturity. And the state of life in my opinion is not like after death. And if this khuntha died after his father while he was a teenager, and a man brought evidence that his father married him (the khuntha) to him (the man) on this endowment for one thousand, and ordered him to pay it to him, and that he urinated from where women urinate, and the heirs denied him, then this has two aspects: either the evidences come together, or one comes before the other. If they come together, then it is either they do not date, or they date and their dates are equal, or one is earlier. If they do not date or date with equal dates, the evidences are all canceled. This is unlike if the husband did not claim half the divorce by divorce before consummation. He only claimed marriage to the khuntha and nothing else, and the rest of the case remains. It is mentioned that the evidence establishing that it is a woman is more deserving. And if they date and one is earlier, the earlier is preferred. If one comes before the other...
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This long passage discusses a woman claiming marriage to a deceased khuntha who died young. The woman brings evidence of the marriage and that the khuntha was male (since she married a man). The brother denies. The judge says he would accept the woman's evidence and treat the khuntha as a boy. If the brother also brings evidence that the khuntha was female, the judge does not accept both and decides by the woman's evidence if they come simultaneously. However, if the husband (the man claiming marriage) brings evidence first and a judgment is passed, subsequent evidence from the woman is not accepted. If both evidence are undated, they cancel. If one is dated earlier, it prevails. Crucially, if the khuntha is still alive, the judge suspends all rulings until the khuntha reaches maturity and signs appear. The commentator emphasizes that the situation during life is not like after death. The passage also mentions a similar case of a man claiming marriage to the khuntha with evidence of female urination. The ruling is that evidence establishing femaleness is given priority (probably because marriage to a female is valid). The principles of precedence of evidence and the suspension of rulings during the khuntha's lifetime are key.

Key Terms
الخنثى المشكل (al-khuntha al-mushkil): An ambiguous hermaphrodite whose gender is not clear even after examination.
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically testimony of two male witnesses or one male and two females.
صداق (sadaq): The dower paid by the husband to the wife in a marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a deceased khuntha, if both the woman (claiming marriage) and the brother bring evidence at the same time, what does the judge accept?
A) The brother's evidence because he is a relative.
B) The woman's evidence because she is the claimant of marriage.
C) Neither, and waits for a sign.
D) Both and divides the inheritance equally.

Key Takeaway
When a woman claims marriage to a deceased khuntha, her evidence of the marriage and male urination is given priority over counter-evidence, but if the khuntha is alive, judgment is suspended until natural signs appear.

Preference of Muslim Testimony in Marriage Claims Involving Dhimmis
Rulings on evidence of marriage and childbirth, and preference of Muslim testimony over dhimmi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that Muslim testimony is preferred over dhimmi testimony in marriage and lineage cases, but equality holds in property disputes.

Core Idea
In marriage and lineage, Muslim testimony prevails; in property, all testimonies are equal.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a nuanced scenario: a marriage claim involving an ambiguous khuntha from the People of the Book. The ruling establishes a critical distinction between family matters and property matters when weighing testimony from Muslims and dhimmis. The judge must prioritize protecting Islamic lineage over procedural equality in family cases, while maintaining neutrality in property disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base scenario
Start with a deceased ambiguous khuntha. A man claims marriage with evidence of contract, dower, and childbirth. Determine how the judge rules on the khuntha's gender and the child's legitimacy.
The judge accepts the evidence and declares the khuntha male (since marriage implies male-female pair) and the child legitimate.
2
Handle conflicting claims without evidence
If the man does not bring evidence but claims the khuntha was female and gave birth, the judge rules the khuntha male by default (paternity requires a male). This shows the default presumption in lineage.
3
Analyze concurrent conflicting evidence
When both parties bring evidence without dates or with equal dates, the evidences cancel each other. If one party's evidence is accepted first and the judge rules, later evidence is rejected. This prevents endless litigation.
This is a procedural rule to avoid contradiction.
4
Apply the preference rule for dhimmi khuntha
If the khuntha is a dhimmi, a Muslim man's claim with dhimmi witnesses is preferred over a dhimmi woman's claim. Similarly, if the man is dhimmi with Muslim witnesses, his evidence prevails. The key is the religion of the claimant, not the witnesses.
The preference is based on the claimant's Islam, not the witnesses' religion.
5
Contrast with property disputes
In property claims, if a Muslim claims property from a dhimmi and brings dhimmi witnesses, the judgment is based on the evidence without preference for the Muslim's religion. This highlights the distinction between family law and property law.
The rule upholds fairness in financial dealings while protecting Islamic family integrity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أن هذا الخنثى المشكل مات قبل أن يظهر أمره فأقام رجل البينة أن أباه زوجها إياه بألف درهم برضاها وأنها ولدت منه هذا الولد قال : أجزت بينته واجعلها امرأته واجعل الولد ابنها. وإن لم يقم هذا الرجل البينة أن أباها زوجها إياه برضا منه وأنه دخل بها وأنها ولدت منه هذا الولد فإنه يقضى بكون الخنثى رجلاً وألزمه الولد، فإن اجتمعت الدعوتان جميعاً وجاءت البينات معاً ولم يوقتا أو وقتا على السواء فإنها تتهاترت البينات جميعاً وجاءت البينات معاً، فإن قامت إحدى هاتين البينتين وقضى القاضي بشهادتهما ثم جاءت البينة الأخرى بعد ذلك قال : لا أقبل البينة الثانية. وإن كان هذا الخنثى المشكل من أهل الكتاب فادعى رجل مسلم أن أباه زوجه إياها على مهر مسمى برضاها وأقام بينة من أهل الكتاب قال : اقضي بينة المسلم وأجعلها امرأته وأبطل بينة المرأة . وكذلك لو كان الرجل من أهل الكتاب وبينته من أهل الإسلام فيقضى للرجل دون المرأة، وهذا بخلاف ما لو وقع الدعوى في المال فادعى المسلم مالاً في يد ذمي وأقام على ذلك شاهدين كتابيين فإنه يقضى بالمال بينهما ولا ترجح إحدى الشهادتين بالإسلام.
And if this ambiguous khuntha died before his matter became apparent, and a man brought evidence that his father married him (the khuntha) to him for a thousand dirhams with her consent, and that she gave birth to this child from him, he said: 'I accept his evidence and make her his wife and make the child his son.' And if this man does not bring evidence that her father married him (the khuntha) to him with his consent, and that he consummated with her, and that she gave birth to this child from him, then it is decided that the khuntha is a man and he is liable for the child. Then if both claims come together and the evidences come together and they do not date or they date equally, then all the evidences cancel each other. And if one of these two evidences stands and the judge rules by their testimony, then the other evidence comes after that, he said: 'I do not accept the second evidence.' And if this ambiguous khuntha is from the People of the Book, and a Muslim man claims that his father married him (the khuntha) to him for a specified dower with her consent, and he brings evidence from the People of the Book, he said: 'I decide by the evidence of the Muslim and make her his wife, and nullify the evidence of the woman.' And similarly, if the man is from the People of the Book and his evidence is from the People of Islam, then judgment is for the man over the woman. And this is contrary to if the claim is about property: if a Muslim claims property in the hand of a dhimmi and brings two dhimmi witnesses, then judgment is given for the property between them, and one testimony is not given preference over the other by Islam.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage covers two main issues. First, when a man claims marriage to a deceased khuntha and presents evidence of marriage and childbirth, the judge accepts the evidence and declares the khuntha male (since a marriage can only be between male and female) and the child legitimate. If the man does not bring evidence but claims the khuntha was female and gave birth, the khuntha is ruled male by default (since paternity requires a male). If both parties bring conflicting evidence without dates, they cancel. Second, if the khuntha is a dhimmi (non-Muslim subject of Islamic state), and a Muslim man claims marriage with dhimmi witnesses, the Muslim's evidence is preferred. However, if the claim is about property, the testimony of dhimmi witnesses for a Muslim is accepted on par with dhimmi witnesses for a dhimmi; there is no preference based on religion in property disputes. This distinction shows that in matters of lineage and marriage, Islam gives preference to Muslims over dhimmis to protect Islamic family structures, but in property, equality is maintained.

Key Terms
أهل الكتاب (ahl al-kitāb): Jews and Christians, who are considered dhimmis under Islamic rule, allowed to practice their religion but subjected to certain restrictions.
الصداق (al-ṣidāq): The dower, a mandatory payment from husband to wife in a marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The judge prefers the Muslim's evidence
C) The evidences cancel each other
D) The judge rejects both evidences

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, Muslim testimony is preferred in marriage and lineage cases to protect Islamic family structures, but in property disputes, all testimony is equal regardless of religion.

Guardian's Acknowledgment in Khuntha Inheritance
Ruling on guardian's acknowledgment in inheritance claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the binding effect of a guardian's acknowledgment of a deceased khuntha's gender on inheritance claims.

Core Idea
A guardian's acknowledgment of a khuntha's gender only binds his own inheritance share, not those of other heirs.

When a khuntha (intersex person) dies, the heirs may disagree on whether the khuntha was male or female. The guardian's acknowledgment (iqrār) does not automatically settle the matter. The Hanafi school carefully limits the effect of such an acknowledgment to prevent injustice to other heirs. This lesson examines the ruling on inheritance claims when the guardian takes a position contrary to the other heirs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Note the case: a khuntha dies after their father. The mother claims the khuntha was male and demands a male's share. The guardian (waṣī) agrees with the mother, but other heirs deny.
2
Locate the ruling
Find the text: 'لا يلزم الورثة الذين أنكروا ميراث الغلام في حقهم ويلزم الوصي المقر ميراث غلام في نصيبه' – the denial by other heirs protects their shares; only the guardian is bound by his acknowledgment.
3
Understand the principle
In Hanafi law, an iqrār (acknowledgment) only binds the one who makes it, not third parties. Here, the guardian's acknowledgment does not prejudice the shares of the other heirs.
This is different from a judicial declaration or evidence.
4
Apply to the living khuntha case
If the khuntha is alive and claims to be male, the guardian's agreement is insufficient; evidence (bayyinah) is required. The guardian's say-so does not override the need for proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو مات أبو الخنثى ثم مات هذا الخنثى فادعت أمه ميراث غلام وأقر الوصي بذلك وجحد بقية الورثة وقالوا هي جارية قال : إذا جاءت الدعوى في الأموال لم يصدق وعلى الأم على ما ادعى. وإن كان هذا الخنثى حياً لم يمت فقال أنا غلام وطلب ميراث غلام من أبيه وصدقه الوصي في ذلك وأنكر بقية الورثة ذلك وقالوا هي جارية قال : لا أعطيه ميراث غلام ولا أصدقه على ذلك إلا ببينة. وإن كان وصيه أخوه زوجه امرأة ثم مات الخنثى وطلبت المرأة ميراثها وقال الوصي هو غلام وقد جاز النكاح ورثت المرأة . جارية لا يلزم الورثة الذي أنكروا ميراث الغلام في حقهم ويلزم الوصي المقر ميراث غلام في نصيبه وترث المرأة من الخنثى ميراث الخنثى من المقر، وإن كان له أخ لأبيه وأمه فأقر أنه جارية وزوجها رجلاً ثم مات الخنثى وقد راهق قبل أن يعلم أنها امرأة وزوجها ثم مات الخنثى قبل أن يعرف حاله فإن النكاح جائز على الأخ الأول وهو الوصي، ولا يجوز على من أنكر من بقية الورثة والنكاح الثاني الذي أقر به الأخر الثاني الذي ليس بوصي باطل في حقه ولا يجوز في حق بقية الورثة . قال : وإن لم يعرف أي النكاحين أول قال أبطل هذا كله ولا أورث شيئاً منهما . وإن عرفت الذي أقر أنها امرأة وزوجها رجلاً أنها أول قال : ألزمه ميراث الأخر في نصيبه ولا ألزم غيره وأبطل النكاح
And if the father of the khuntha died, then this khuntha died, and his mother claimed the inheritance of a boy, and the guardian acknowledged that, but the rest of the heirs denied and said she is a girl, he said: 'When the claim is about property, he is not believed, and the mother is upon what she claims.' And if this khuntha is alive and has not died, and he said 'I am a boy' and demanded the inheritance of a boy from his father, and the guardian agreed with him in that, but the rest of the heirs denied that and said she is a girl, he said: 'I do not give him the inheritance of a boy, and I do not believe him in that except with evidence.' And if his guardian is his brother, he married a woman to him, then the khuntha died, and the woman demanded her inheritance, and the guardian said he is a boy and the marriage is valid, the woman inherits. A girl: it does not bind the heirs who denied the inheritance of a boy in their portion, and it binds the guardian who acknowledged the inheritance of a boy in his share, and the woman inherits from the khuntha the inheritance of the khuntha from the acknowledge. And if he had a consanguine brother, and he acknowledged that she is a girl and married her to a man, then the khuntha died after having reached puberty before it was known that she was a woman, and then the khuntha died before his condition was known, then the marriage is valid on the first brother who is the guardian, and it is not valid on those who denied from the rest of the heirs, and the second marriage which the second brother acknowledged, who is not the guardian, is invalid in his regard and is not valid in the regard of the rest of the heirs. He said: 'And if the order of the two marriages is not known, I nullify all this and do not make any inheritance from them.' And if it is known which one acknowledged that she is a woman and married her to a man was first, he said: 'I impose the inheritance of the other in his share, and I do not impose on others, and I nullify the marriage.'
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage deals with the effects of a guardian's (waṣī) acknowledgment of the khuntha's gender. In inheritance claims after death, if the guardian acknowledges the khuntha was male, this acknowledgment only affects his own share of the inheritance, not the shares of other heirs who deny. If the khuntha is alive and claims to be male, the guardian's agreement is not sufficient; evidence is required. In marriage contracts: if a guardian (who is a brother) marries the khuntha as a woman to a man, and the khuntha dies, the marriage is valid only as to the guardian's share; other heirs are not bound. If two brothers each contract a marriage with different claims, the earlier valid marriage takes precedence; if order is unknown, both are nullified. This shows the principle that a guardian's action cannot prejudice the rights of other heirs who have not consented.

Key Terms
الوصي (al-waṣī): The guardian or executor appointed to manage the affairs of a minor or an estate.
المقر (al-muqirr): The person who acknowledges or confesses to a fact, here the guardian acknowledging the khuntha's gender.
إقرار (iqrār): Acknowledgment or confession of a fact or right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The entire estate is treated as male because the guardian acknowledged.
B) The guardian's share is treated as male; the other heirs' shares remain as per their claim.
C) The mother receives a male share from the entire estate.
D) The case is suspended until a medical examination determines gender.

Key Takeaway
A guardian's acknowledgment of a khuntha's gender in inheritance claims is only binding on the guardian's own share.

Validity of Marriage Contracts for Khuntha by Guardian
Rulings on marriage contracts for khuntha and their validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of marriage contracts for a khuntha when the guardian's acknowledgment of gender conflicts with other heirs.

Core Idea
A guardian's marriage contract for a khuntha is valid only as to the guardian's own share; other heirs are not bound and the contract may be null if the order of conflicting contracts is unknown.

When a khuntha is married by a guardian who acknowledges one gender, but other heirs claim a different gender, the validity of the marriage is partial. The Hanafi school protects the rights of all parties by limiting the effect of the guardian's actions. This lesson explores the rulings on marriage contracts for khuntha when multiple claims exist.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
There are three cases: (1) guardian (brother) marries khuntha as a male to a woman; khuntha dies; woman claims inheritance; guardian says male. (2) Two brothers: one guardian marries as female, another non-guardian marries as female. (3) The rulings on order.
The text: 'إن كان له أخ لأبيه وأمه فأقر أنه جارية وزوجها رجلاً' – the first brother (guardian) acknowledges female and marries; the second brother (non-guardian) also does so.
2
Understand the partial validity
The guardian's marriage contract is considered valid for the guardian himself (meaning he is bound by it regarding inheritance from the khuntha), but it does not bind other heirs who deny the gender. Similarly, a marriage by a non-guardian brother is invalid for him and does not bind others.
This reflects the principle that acts of a guardian cannot prejudice non-consenting heirs.
3
Apply the rule of unknown order
If the sequence of two marriages is unknown, both are nullified (أبطل هذا كله ولا أورث شيئاً منهما). This prevents uncertainty in inheritance and marital status.
4
Apply the rule of known order
If it is known which acknowledgment and marriage came first, that marriage is upheld. The guardian who made the later invalid marriage is liable for the inheritance of the other in his share (ألزمه ميراث الأخر في نصيبه).

Source Text

A guardian's marriage contract for a khuntha is only binding on himself; if order is unknown, all such marriages are nullified.

Ruling When Two Ambiguous Khunthas Marry and Die
Ruling on conflicting inheritance after a marriage of two ambiguous hermaphrodites

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two ambiguous khunthas marry each other and die, and explain why the judge does not decide between conflicting evidence.

Core Idea
When gender is inherently ambiguous, the judge cannot resolve conflicting inheritance claims.

This lesson examines a unique Hanafi case where two ambiguous hermaphrodites (khuntha mushkil) marry each other. Upon their deaths, each set of heirs claims their relative was the husband, creating contradictory evidence. The judge's response—refusing to decide—reveals a key principle: where essential facts (gender) are indeterminable, the court refrains from ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the basis of marriage
The case involves two khuntha mushkil (ambiguous hermaphrodites) who married on the assumption one is male and the other female. Their gender is not known.
‘Khuntha mushkil’ means a person whose gender cannot be determined by physical signs or urination.
2
Understand the conflicting claims after death
When both die, each side’s heirs claim that their deceased relative was the husband (and therefore entitled to inherit as a husband). Both sides bring evidence (bayyinah).
The conflict is inherent: each heir’s claim logically contradicts the other.
3
State the judge’s ruling
The judge says: ‘I do not decide anything of that.’ He refuses to rule on which evidence is correct.
The Arabic: 'لا أقضي بشيء من ذلك'
4
Analyze the rationale
The judge cannot decide because the fundamental fact—the gender of each khuntha—is unknown and cannot be determined at this point. Without knowing who was the husband, inheritance cannot be assigned.
This reflects the Hanafi principle that a judge must have certainty (yaqin) or strong probability (ghalabat al-zann) before ruling.
5
Consider the cut-off continuation
The text adds: 'And if one of the two evidences comes before, he accepts it from him, and the rest of the heirs said: she...' The text is incomplete, but it suggests that if one evidence is presented first, the judge may accept it, pending a possible later contradiction.
The ruling may continue on the next page; we only have this partial statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خنثى مشكل مراهق وخنثى مثله مشكل تزوج أحدهما صاحبه على أن أحدهما رجل والآخر امرأة إذا مات وأقام كل واحد من ورثتهما بينة أنه هو الزوج وأن الآخر هو الزوجة قال : لا أقضي بشيء من ذلك. وإن جاءت إحدى البينتين قبل منه وقال بقية الورثة هي
A pubertal ambiguous khuntha and another similar ambiguous khuntha married each other on the basis that one is a man and the other is a woman. When they die, and each of their heirs brings evidence that he (the deceased) is the husband and the other is the wife, he said: 'I do not decide anything of that.' And if one of the two evidences comes before, he accepts it from him, and the rest of the heirs said: she...
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This final excerpt addresses a unique case where two ambiguous khunthas marry each other, each claiming to be a male marrying a female. When they die, heirs on each side claim their relative was the husband (and therefore inherits as a husband). The judge refuses to decide because the evidence is inherently contradictory and the genders are unknown. If one evidence comes first, he accepts it, but the text is cut off. The principle is that in cases of extreme ambiguity, the judge refrains from issuing a ruling. This demonstrates the Hanafi reluctance to rule on matters where the essential facts (gender) are indeterminable.

Key Terms
خنثى مشكل (khunthā mushkil): An ambiguous hermaphrodite whose gender cannot be determined by physical signs or urination patterns.
مراهق (murāhiq): An adolescent who has reached puberty or is close to it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He decides based on the majority of witnesses.
B) He accepts the evidence of the first to present it.
C) He does not decide anything.
D) He asks for medical examination of the bodies.

Key Takeaway
When gender is inherently ambiguous, the Hanafi judge refrains from ruling on inheritance claims.

Marriage and Paternity Claims After Khuntha's Death
Ruling with proof: evidence determines gender in claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a man claims marriage to a deceased khuntha and identify the conditions for acceptance or rejection of evidence.

Core Idea
Evidence determines gender for a deceased khuntha: proof of marriage makes her a wife; no proof makes him a father.

In this lesson, we examine a unique ruling from the Hanafi school concerning a deceased person of ambiguous gender (khuntha mushkil). A man claims he married the khuntha as a woman and she bore his child. The text lays out how evidence (bayyinah) resolves the claims and how conflicting evidence is handled. This ruling highlights the interplay between default presumptions and the power of proof in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The khuntha dies before its gender becomes apparent. A man claims to have married her and that she gave birth to his child.
The khuntha is ambiguous; its legal gender at death is not known.
2
Distinguish the two sub-cases
Case 1: The man brings evidence (bayyinah) of marriage and paternity. Case 2: The man does not bring such evidence.
Evidence must include witnesses that the father married her with her consent and that the child was born from that marriage.
3
Determine the ruling for each sub-case
With evidence: the qadi accepts it, declares the khuntha a wife, and the child is legitimate. Without evidence: the qadi deems the khuntha a man and imposes the child on him (i.e., the child belongs to the khuntha).
This default to male without evidence is a Hanafi position.
4
Analyze the handling of conflicting evidence
If both parties (the man and perhaps another claimant or the khuntha's estate) bring evidence simultaneously, all evidence is nullified. If one evidence is ruled upon first, later evidence is rejected.
This prevents re-litigation and promotes finality of judgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أن هذا الخنثى المشكل مات قبل أن يظهر أمره فأقام رجل البينة أن أباه زوجها إياه بألف درهم برضاها وأنها ولدت منه هذا الولد قال : أجزت بينته واجعلها امرأته واجعل الولد ابنها. وإن لم يقم هذا الرجل البينة أن أباها زوجها إياه برضا منه وأنه دخل بها وأنها ولدت منه هذا الولد فإنه يقضى بكون الخنثى رجلاً وألزمه الولد، فإن اجتمعت الدعوتان جميعاً وجاءت البينات معاً ولم يوقتا أو وقتا على السواء فإنها تتهاترت البينات جميعاً وجاءت البينات معاً، فإن قامت إحدى هاتين البينتين وقضى القاضي بشهادتهما ثم جاءت البينة الأخرى بعد ذلك قال : لا أقبل البينة الثانية.
And if this ambiguous khuntha died before his matter became apparent, and a man brought evidence that her father married her to him for a thousand dirhams with her consent and that she gave birth to this child from him, he said: I accept his evidence and make her his wife and make the child his son. And if this man did not bring evidence that her father married her to him with his consent and that he entered her and that she gave birth to this child from him, then judgment is made that the khuntha is a man and the child is imposed on him. If both claims come together and evidence comes together and they did not date or dated equally, then all evidence is nullified and evidence comes together. If one of these two evidences stands and the qadi rules by their testimony, then the other evidence comes after that, he said: I do not accept the second evidence.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with implications for marriage and paternity. It addresses a scenario where a khuntha (ambiguous gender) dies before gender is determined. A man claims he married the khuntha (as a woman) and that she bore his child. If he provides proof (witnesses) that her father arranged the marriage with her consent and that she gave birth, the qadi accepts his evidence, declares the khuntha his wife, and the child is his. If he lacks proof, the khuntha is judged to be male (since the default for an ambiguous khuntha in inheritance etc. is to give the lesser share, but here for marriage claims, if no proof, it is deemed a man to impose the child on him? This is a technical point). If both parties bring evidence simultaneously, the evidence is nullified, and if one evidence is ruled upon before the other, the later is rejected.

Key Terms
الخنثى المشكل (al-khuntha al-mushkil): A person with ambiguous genitalia such that the gender (male/female) cannot be determined.
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence (witnesses) required to prove a claim.
تهاتر (tahatur): Mutual nullification of evidence when conflicting claims are brought equally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man claims marriage to a deceased khuntha and cannot provide evidence, what is the ruling?
A) The khuntha is declared a woman and the man must pay dower
B) The khuntha is declared a man and the child is imposed on him
C) The case is dismissed
D) The khuntha's estate is divided equally

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, evidence (bayyinah) determines the legal gender of a deceased ambiguous person for marriage and paternity claims; conflicting evidence leads to nullification.

Marriage by Executor and Inheritance of Khuntha's Wife
Ruling on validity and inheritance in khuntha's multiple marriages by executors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a marriage performed by an executor for a khuntha and determine the inheritance consequences when the khuntha's gender is disputed among heirs.

Core Idea
The executor's confession about the khuntha's gender binds only his share, not the other heirs.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a layered scenario involving a khuntha (hermaphrodite) whose gender is uncertain. An executor who is a brother marries the khuntha to a woman. After the khuntha's death, the wife claims inheritance, and the executor's gender assessment determines the validity of the marriage. The rulings carefully distinguish between the executor's personal liability and the position of other heirs, highlighting the principle that a confession (iqrar) is binding only on the confessor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their roles
In the first scenario, the executor (brother) marries the khuntha to a woman. The khuntha dies, and the woman claims inheritance. The executor states the khuntha was male (validating marriage) or female (invalidating).
The executor's statement is a confession about gender.
2
Apply the iqrar principle to inheritance
If the executor says 'boy', the marriage is valid and the woman inherits from the khuntha. If 'girl', the marriage is invalid. The executor's confession binds only his own share, not those of other heirs who deny.
Iqrar is binding only on the confessor.
3
Analyze the second scenario with two brothers
One brother is executor and marries the khuntha to a man (considering khuntha as female). Another brother (non-executor) also marries the same khuntha. The first marriage is valid for the executor but not for others. The second marriage is void in the second brother's right and all heirs.
The executor's authority and confession matter.
4
Determine consequences when order is unknown
If it is unknown which marriage occurred first, all marriages are annulled and no inheritance is given from either. If the first marriage is known to be that of the executor, he is liable for inheritance in his share, and the marriage is annulled (since the khuntha's gender is uncertain).
Certainty overrides uncertainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان وصيه أخوه زوجه امرأة ثم مات الخنثى وطلبت المرأة ميراثها وقال الوصي هو غلام وقد جاز النكاح ورثت المرأة . جارية لا يلزم الورثة الذي أنكروا ميراث الغلام في حقهم ويلزم الوصي المقر ميراث غلام في نصيبه وترث المرأة من الخنثى ميراث الخنثى من المقر، وإن كان له أخ لأبيه وأمه فأقر أنه جارية وزوجها رجلاً ثم مات الخنثى وقد راهق قبل أن يعلم أنها امرأة وزوجها ثم مات الخنثى قبل أن يعرف حاله فإن النكاح جائز على الأخ الأول وهو الوصي، ولا يجوز على من أنكر من بقية الورثة والنكاح الثاني الذي أقر به الأخر الثاني الذي ليس بوصي باطل في حقه ولا يجوز في حق بقية الورثة . قال : وإن لم يعرف أي النكاحين أول قال أبطل هذا كله ولا أورث شيئاً منهما . وإن عرفت الذي أقر أنها امرأة وزوجها رجلاً أنها أول قال : ألزمه ميراث الأخر في نصيبه ولا ألزم غيره وأبطل النكاح
And if his executor is his brother, and he married him to a woman, then the khuntha died, and the woman sought her inheritance, and the executor said he is a boy and the marriage is valid, the woman inherits. But if [they said] girl, it does not bind the heirs who denied the inheritance of a boy in their right, and the confessing executor is bound by the inheritance of a boy in his share, and the woman inherits from the khuntha the inheritance of the khuntha from the confessor. And if he had a full brother, and he (the brother) affirmed that she is a girl and married her to a man, then the khuntha died, and he had approached puberty before it was known that she is a woman and she was married, then the khuntha died before his status was known, then the marriage is valid regarding the first brother who is the executor, and not valid regarding those who denied from the rest of the heirs. The second marriage affirmed by the second brother who is not an executor is void in his right and not valid regarding the rest of the heirs. He said: and if it is not known which of the two marriages was first, he said: I annul all of this and do not give inheritance from either. And if it is known that the one who affirmed that she is a woman and married her to a man was first, he said: I impose on him the inheritance of the other in his share and do not impose on others, and I annul the marriage.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on multiple marriages and inheritances involving a khuntha. The executor (wasi) who is the brother marries the khuntha (as a woman) to a man. After the khuntha's death, the wife claims inheritance. If the executor says the khuntha was a boy (so the marriage is valid, as male-female marriage), the woman inherits. If the executor says girl, the marriage is invalid. The ruling distinguishes between the executor's own share: the executor's confession binds him (he must give inheritance from his share) but not other heirs. Then a scenario with two brothers: one executor marries the khuntha as a woman to a man; another brother (not executor) later marries the same khuntha (as a woman?) to another man. The first marriage is valid for the executor, the second is void. If the order is unknown, all are annulled. If the first marriage is known to be the one affirmed by the first brother (executor), then the executor is liable for inheritance in his share, and the marriage is annulled (since it turns out the khuntha is not definitively a woman).

Key Terms
وصي (wasi): Executor of the will; here the brother appointed as guardian.
راهق (rahq): Approaching the age of puberty (murahiq).
أقر (aqarra): Affirmed or confessed (about the khuntha's gender).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Both marriages are valid
B) The first marriage is valid
C) All marriages are annulled and no inheritance is given
D) The executor's marriage is valid for all heirs

Key Takeaway
In marriages involving a khuntha, the executor's gender affirmation determines validity only for his own share, and uncertainty about the order of marriages leads to annulment and no inheritance.

Ruling on Marriage of Two Ambiguous Khunthas and Conflicting Evidence
Ruling on marriage between ambiguous khunthas and conflicting evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two ambiguous khunthas marry and their heirs bring conflicting evidence about who was husband and wife, and explain the qadi's response to simultaneous vs. sequential evidence.

Core Idea
Fundamental uncertainty in the marriage renders the qadi powerless to decide.

This passage addresses a uniquely complex scenario in nikah: two individuals whose gender is ambiguous (khuntha mushkil) marry each other, each assuming the other is of the opposite gender. After their deaths, the heirs of each claim that their deceased was the husband. How should a qadi handle such conflicting evidence? The Hanafi ruling provides a clear procedural answer rooted in the principle that the qadi cannot rule on a matter where the very basis is uncertain.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Parties and the Marriage
Understand that both parties are ambiguous khunthas (khuntha mushkil) near puberty (murahiq), and they married each other on the assumption that one is male and the other female.
The marriage is inherently uncertain because the genders are unknown.
2
Recognize the Conflicting Evidence
After their deaths, each heir presents evidence that his deceased was the husband and the other was the wife. The evidence is contradictory.
The qadi faces two competing claims with no way to verify.
3
Understand the Qadi's Response to Simultaneous Evidence
When both evidences arrive at the same time, the qadi does not rule on anything (لا أقضي بشيء من ذلك). He rejects both because the basis of the marriage is fundamentally uncertain.
The marriage is considered void ab initio due to the unknown genders.
4
Understand the Qadi's Response to Sequential Evidence
If one evidence arrives first and the qadi rules accordingly, then later the second evidence arrives, the first ruling is upheld and the second evidence is annulled.
This is based on the principle that a qadi's ruling, once issued, is not overturned by subsequent evidence.
5
Extract the Legal Principle
The key principle: in cases where the subject of the marriage is inherently uncertain (due to ambiguous gender), no ruling can be made on conflicting evidence. If a ruling is made first, it stands.
This shows the Hanafi insistence on certainty (yaqin) in judicial decisions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خنثى مشكل مراهق وخنثى مثله مشكل تزوج أحدهما صاحبه على أن أحدهما رجل والآخر امرأة إذا مات وأقام كل واحد من ورثتهما بينة أنه هو الزوج وأن الآخر هو الزوجة قال : لا أقضي بشيء من ذلك. وإن جاءت إحدى البينتين قبل الأخرى وقضى بها ثم جاءت البينة الثانية قال : أبطل البينة الأخرى وقضاء الأول ماض على حاله
An ambiguous khuntha who is near puberty and another like him ambiguous, one of them married the other on the basis that one is a man and the other a woman. If they died, and each of their heirs brought evidence that he is the husband and the other is the wife, he said: I do not rule on anything of that. And if one of the two evidences came before the other and he ruled by it, then the second evidence came, he said: I annul the other evidence and the first ruling remains as it is.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on a problematic marriage: two khunthas (both ambiguous in gender) marry each other, assuming opposite genders. After they die, the heirs of each claim that their deceased was the husband and the other the wife. The qadi does not accept any such evidence because the basis of the marriage is inherently uncertain. If one evidence comes first and a ruling is made, then the second comes later, the first ruling is upheld and the second is rejected. This reinforces the principle that once a qadi rules, later evidence is not accepted.

Key Terms
خنثى مشكل (khuntha mushkil): An individual whose gender is ambiguous and cannot be determined by external signs.
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically testimony of two witnesses.
قضاء (qada): Judicial ruling, judgment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He rules in favor of the first evidence
B) He divides the inheritance equally
C) He does not rule on anything
D) He asks for physical examination of the bodies

Key Takeaway
In cases of fundamental uncertainty about the marriage, the qadi will not rule on conflicting evidence; if a ruling was made first, it is upheld.

Gesture and Writing of a Mute Are Like Clear Expression
Ruling on mute's gesture and writing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the legal validity of a mute's gesture and writing, and distinguish it from the case of a tongue-tied person.

Core Idea
A mute's gesture and writing are as legally binding as spoken words.

The Hanafi school grants full legal capacity to mutes in transactions and family law. This short but powerful ruling from al-Kanz al-Daqa'iq states that a mute's gesture or writing is equivalent to clear speech. However, it also introduces an exception: a person who is temporarily tongue-tied does not have the same privilege. Understanding this ruling requires knowing who qualifies as 'akhras' and the rationale behind treating gesture as speech.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and the ruling
The text begins with 'ima' al-akhras wa kitabatuhu ka al-bayan' — the gesture and writing of a mute are like clear expression. Identify the subject (the mute) and the ruling (equivalence to speech).
The ruling is a general principle, not limited to a specific contract.
2
Understand the exception
The phrase 'bikhilafi mu'taqal al-lisan' introduces an exception: a tongue-tied person is different. Note the contrast: akhras (mute) vs mu'taqal al-lisan (temporarily speechless).
The exception is mentioned in the context of will (wasiyya) and marriage (nikah), but likely applies to all transactions.
3
Apply the ruling to a specific contract
Given a mute person performing a marriage contract (nikah) through gestures, determine the validity. According to the text, it is valid like clear expression.
This ruling is based on necessity: speech is not possible, so gesture and writing substitute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (إيماء الأخرس وكتابته كالبيان بخلاف معتقل اللسان في وصيته ونكاح
He said (may Allah have mercy on him): 'The gesture of the mute and his writing are like clear expression, unlike a tongue-tied person in his will and marriage...'
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage states a foundational ruling: a person who is mute (akhras) can effect legal transactions (like marriage, divorce, sale) through gesture (ima') or writing (kitaba), and these are given the same legal effect as verbal expression (bayan). The exception is a person who is 'mu'taqal al-lisan' (tongue-tied, i.e., unable to speak due to a temporary condition), but not permanently mute; for such a person, gesture and writing may not be equivalent. The text is incomplete, but the principle is clear.

Key Terms
إيماء الأخرس (ima' al-akhras): The gesture of a mute person that is understood by others
معتقل اللسان (mu'taqal al-lisan): A person whose tongue is tied or temporarily unable to speak (not a congenital mute)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is invalid because marriage requires verbal expression.
B) It is valid and equivalent to clear speech.
C) It is valid only if witnessed by two doctors.
D) It is valid only if the gesture is repeated twice.

Key Takeaway
A mute's gesture and writing are legally equivalent to speech, but a tongue-tied person must still try to speak or write.

Validity of Marriage Contracts Using Persian Terms
Rulings on validity of marriage contracts using Persian words

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish valid from invalid marriage offers in any language by analyzing whether the terms indicate explicit offer and acceptance.

Core Idea
A marriage contract requires clear offer and acceptance in any language; ambiguous suitability statements do not suffice.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq, the commentator examines three Persian exchanges to illustrate when a marriage contract is validly concluded. The key principle is that offer (ījāb) and acceptance (qabūl) must be explicit, regardless of language. Understanding these examples will sharpen your ability to evaluate contract language in non-Arabic contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure of each exchange
Determine whether there is a clear offer (e.g., 'I make you my wife') and a clear acceptance (e.g., 'I accept'). If the words merely ask about suitability, no contract is formed.
The first example lacks both; the third lacks acceptance from the intended spouse.
2
Translate and parse the key Persian terms
Understand the meaning of words like 'shadam' (I became), 'kardanidam' (I made), and 'dashtam' (I made/have). The grammatical form helps determine whether the speaker is performing the act or describing a state.
The commentator provides detailed parsing to show that some forms are declarative and others interrogative.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule on language
Hanafi law allows marriage in any language if the meaning is clear. Check if the words used would be understood by the parties as an actual marriage contract.
Other schools may require Arabic if possible, but this passage follows the Hanafi view.
4
Classify each example as valid or invalid
Based on the first three steps, categorize each exchange: the first is invalid (no clear offer/acceptance), the second is valid (both present), and the third is invalid (only suitability mentioned).
Memorize the three examples as benchmarks for similar cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : توزن من شدى يعني أنت صرت زوجة لي (فقالت المرأة شدم) يعني صرت لم ينعقد النكاح لأن هذا لا يدل على الإيجاب والقبول فقوله «تو» بضم التاء المثناة فوق وسكون الواو معناه أنت وقوله «زن» بفتح الزاي المعجمة والنون هو اسم للمرأة وقوله «من» بفتح الميم والنون ومعناه أنا وقوله شدم بضم الشين المعجمة وفتح الدال المهلمة في آخره میم آخر الحروف ساكنة معناه صرت، وهذه اللفظة تتصرف كاللفظ العربي فمصدره شدن والماضي شد والمضارع شود إذا أريد الإخبار عن الجمع يقال شديم بكسر الدال وزيادة الياء آخر الحروف بعد الدال قبل ميم المتكلم. قال رحمه الله : (ولو قال رجل لامرأة خويشتن رازن من كردا يندي معناه هل جعلت نفسك لي زوجة فقالت المرأة في جوابه كردا يندم يعني جعلت وقال الرجل بزير فتم يعني قبلت ينعقد النكاح متمم لاشتماله على الإيجاب والقبول. وقله «خویشتن يؤدي معنى نفسك وهو بكسر الخاء المعجمة يكتب بالواو بعدها من غير أن يتلفظ بها وكذلك الياء بعد الواو وشين معجمة ساكنة بعدها تاء مثناة من فوق مفتوحة وفي آخره نون. وقوله (را) بفتح الراء بعدها ألف ساكنة تؤدي معنى التخصيص الإشارة بها وهي مفعول. وقوله «من» يعني أنا وقوله كردانيدي» بالكاف الصماء المفتوحة والراء الساكنة والدال المفتوحة والنون المكسورة لا بعدها ألف وبعدها ياء ساكنة ودال مهملة مكسورة وفي آخره ياء أخرى ساكنة وهذه للخطاب تؤدي معنى الجعل والتصيير. وقوله «كردايندم» كذلك إلا أنه للمتكلم وحده، وكذلك للمخاطبة إذا زيد ياء بعد الدال مثل «كردانيدي» وإذا أريد جميع المخاطب يزاد بعد الدال ياء المخاطب مثل كردانيديد وإذا أريد المتكلم مع الغير يزاد فيه ياء بعد الدال وقبل الميم ويقال كردانيديم». وقوله بزيرفتم بفتح الباء الصماء يكون مخرجه قريب من مخرج الفاء وبكسر الزاي المعجمة بعدها باء ساكنة وبعدها راء مفتوحة وبعدها فاء ساكنة وبعدها تاء مثناة من فوق مفتوحة وفي آخره ميم ساكنة . قال رحمه الله : (ولو) قال رجل لآخر دوختر خویشتن را بيسر من ارزاني داشتي) معناه هل جعلت ابنتك لائقة لأبني فقال أبو البنت في جوابه داشتم يعني جعلت لا ينعقد النكاح لأنه ليس بمشتمل على الإيجاب والقبول ولا يلزم من جعل ابنته لائقة لابنه حصول العقد بينهما. قوله «دختر بضم الدال المهملة وسكون الخاء المعجمة وفتح التاء المثناة فوق وفي آخره راء معناه البنت وقوله بيسر لفظان مركبان الأول لفظ باء الموحدة يؤدي معنى لام.
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'Tuzan man shad? means you became my wife? (and the woman said 'shadam' meaning 'I became') the marriage is not concluded because this does not indicate offer and acceptance. So his word 'tu' (ضم التاء المثناة فوق وسكون الواو) means 'you', and 'zan' (فتح الزاي والنون) is a woman, and 'man' (فتح الميم والنون) means 'I', and 'shadam' (ضم الشين وفتح الدال وآخره ميم ساكنة) means 'I became'. This word declines like an Arabic word: its masdar is 'shadan', past 'shad', present 'shavad'. For plural, 'shadim' (كسر الدال وزيادة ياء). He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And if a man says to a woman 'Khuish-tan razan man kardanidi?' meaning 'Did you make yourself a wife for me?' and the woman replies 'kardanidam' meaning 'I made', and the man says 'bazir fatham' meaning 'I accept', the marriage is concluded completely because it contains offer and acceptance. His word 'khwish-tan' means 'yourself', written with khā, wāw, yā, shīn, tā, nūn. 'Ra' indicates specification and is the object. 'Man' means 'I'. 'Kardanidi' is the second person form meaning 'you made'. 'Kardanidam' is first person. 'Bazir fatham' means 'I accept'. He said: (And if a man says to another 'Dukhtar khwish-tan ra baisar man arzani dashti?' meaning 'Have you made your daughter suitable for my son?' and the father of the girl replies 'dashtam' meaning 'I have made', the marriage is not concluded because it does not contain offer and acceptance, and making a daughter suitable does not necessitate a contract. The word 'dukhtar' means daughter, and 'baisar' means for my son.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage is a parsing exercise where the commentator examines the validity of marriage contracts when Persian words are used. The author presents three examples. First, a man says 'Tuzan man shad?' (You became my wife?) and the woman says 'Shadam' (I became). This is invalid because it does not contain clear offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul). Second, a man asks 'Did you make yourself a wife for me?' and the woman replies 'I made,' then the man says 'I accept.' This is valid because it includes offer and acceptance. Third, a man asks the father 'Have you made your daughter suitable for my son?' and the father says 'I have made.' This is invalid because it merely indicates suitability, not a binding offer. The commentator explains the Persian words in detail, showing that the grammatical forms correspond to Arabic patterns and are understandable. The key principle is that marriage requires explicit offer and acceptance, regardless of language, but the words must clearly indicate the intent to conclude a marriage contract.

Key Terms
إيجاب (ījāb): Offer in a marriage contract; must be clear and explicit.
قبول (qabūl): Acceptance; must immediately follow offer and be clear.
انعقاد النكاح (in‘iqād al-nikāḥ): Conclusion of a valid marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Man: 'Tuzan man shad?' Woman: 'Shadam'
B) Man: 'Khuish-tan razan man kardanidi?' Woman: 'Kardanidam' Man: 'Bazir fatham'
C) Man: 'Dukhtar khwish-tan ra baisar man arzani dashti?' Father: 'Dashtam'
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
A valid marriage contract in Hanafi law requires explicit offer and acceptance in any language; mere talk of suitability does not constitute a contract.

Validity of Persian Marriage Contracts: Clear Offer and Acceptance Required
Explaining which Persian phrases constitute valid offer and acceptance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Persian marriage phrases presented, identify which one contains explicit offer and acceptance, and apply the Hanafi rule that unambiguous mutual consent is required for a valid marriage contract.

Core Idea
A marriage contract in any language requires explicit offer and acceptance; ambiguous phrases do not create a valid marriage.

What happens when a marriage proposal is made in Persian rather than Arabic? The Hanafi school permits marriage contracts in any language, but insists that the words clearly convey mutual consent. This lesson examines three Persian phrases from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq to see which ones create a valid marriage and why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three phrases
Read the source passage and locate the three Persian examples. Note the exact wording and the responses given.
The first phrase is 'توزن من شدى', the second is 'خويشتن رازن من كردا يندي' with response, and the third is 'دوختر خویشتن را بيسر من ارزاني داشتي'.
2
Determine the presence of offer and acceptance
For each phrase, decide whether it contains a clear offer (ijab) from one party and a matching, explicit acceptance (qabul) from the other. Consult the commentary on the Persian words.
The second phrase includes 'كردا يندم' (I made) and 'بزير فتم' (I accepted), which are explicit. The others lack such clarity.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle
State the Hanafi rule: a marriage contract is valid only when the offer and acceptance are unambiguous and mirror each other. Conclude which scenarios produce a valid marriage.
Only the second scenario yields a valid marriage (sahih); the first and third are invalid (batil) because they do not show clear mutual consent.

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the validity of marriage contracts conducted using Persian phrases. It presents three scenarios: first, the phrase 'توزن من شدى' is ambiguous and does not constitute a valid marriage because it lacks clear offer and acceptance; the woman's response 'شدم' does not remedy this. Second, the phrase 'خويشتن رازن من كردا يندي' with proper offer and acceptance (the woman says 'كردا يندم' meaning 'I made' and the man says 'بزير فتم' meaning 'I accepted') results in a valid marriage. Third, the phrase 'دوختر خویشتن را بيسر من ارزاني داشتي' is not a marriage contract but a statement of suitability; it does not create a marriage even if the father says 'داشتم' (I made). The key principle is that valid offer and acceptance must be clear and mutual, using words that explicitly convey the marriage contract. The commentary explains the meanings of each Persian term to clarify the legal analysis.

Key Terms
إيجاب (ījāb): The offer made by one party in a marriage contract.
قبول (qabūl): The acceptance of the offer in a marriage contract.
عقد النكاح (ʿaqd an-nikāḥ): The marriage contract, which requires offer and acceptance.

Hanafi Rulings on Wife's Nushuz and Exceptions
Commentary on nushuz rulings with exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi conditions for nushuz and identify when a wife's refusal is excused, preserving her maintenance rights.

Core Idea
Nushuz requires unjustified withholding; legitimate excuses like haram residence or enforcing a right prevent nushuz.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the base text's ruling that a wife who prevents her husband from entering her house while he lives there is nashiz (disobedient) and loses maintenance. The commentary clarifies exceptions: if the husband lives in a usurped house (ghasb), her refusal is justified, and if she prevents him to enforce her right to separate residence in his house, it is not nushuz. Additionally, she cannot demand a separate dwelling from his slave girl because he needs her service.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The base ruling: If a wife prevents her husband from entering her house while he lives with her there, she is nashiz. This triggers loss of maintenance (nafaqa). Note that this applies only when she prevents him unjustly.
The ruling is based on the principle that withholding oneself without right nullifies maintenance.
2
Check for legitimate excuses
The wife is not nashiz if her prevention is justified. The passage lists three scenarios: (1) She prevents him to enforce her right to reside in his house (her maintenance continues). (2) He lives in a usurped house, so she is right to refuse. (3) She wants a separate house from his slave girl – this is not a valid excuse.
Contrast with cases where she confines herself for a debt or is kidnapped – those do not excuse nushuz.
3
Apply the usurped house exception
If the husband resides in a house taken by ghasb (usurpation), the wife may refuse to live there without becoming nashiz, because living in a usurped house is haram. Her right to maintenance remains intact as the shortcoming is from his side.
This is a clear case where the wife's refusal is based on a shari' obligation to avoid sin.
4
Apply the slave girl exception
If the wife demands a separate dwelling from the husband's slave girl, she has no right to do so. The husband requires the slave girl for service, and he cannot be prevented from having her in the same house. Thus, her demand does not justify nushuz.
This highlights that the wife's rights are balanced against the husband's needs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و هو يسكن معها في بيتها نشوز ولو سكن في بيت الغصب فامتنع لا تكون ناشزة قالت لا أسكن مع أمتك وأريد بيتاً على حدة وليس لها ذلك قال رحمه الله : (منعها) كلام أضافي مبتدأ أي منع المرأة زوجها (عن الدخول عليها و الحال أنه (هو) أي الزوج يسكن معها في بيتها نشوز) لأنها حبست نفسها منه بغير حق فلا تجب النفقة لها ما دامت على منعه فيتحقق النشوز منها فصار كحبسها نفسها في منزل غيرها. هذا إذا منعته ومرادها السكنى في منزلها، وإن كان المنع لينقلها إلى منزله لا تكون ناشزة لأن السكنى واجبة لها عليه فكان حبسها نفسها منه بحق فلا تسقط نفقتها لأن التقصير جاء من جهته فصار كما إذا حبست نفسها لاستيفاء مهرها بخلاف ما إذا حبست بسبب دين عليها أو غصبها غاصب وذهب بها لأن الفوات ليس من قبله، وبخلاف ما إذا كانت ساكنة معه في منزله ولم تمكنه من الوطء لأنه يمكنه الوطء كرهاً غالباً رحمه الله: (ولو سكن في بيت الغصب فامتنع لا تكون ناشزة) لأنها محقة لأن السكنى فيه حرام قال رحمه الله : (قالت لا أسكن مع أمتك وأريد بيتاً على حدة وليس لها ذلك) لأنه لا بد له ممن يخدمه فلا يمكن منعه من ذلك.
And he lives with her in her house, it is nushuz. And if he lives in a usurped house and she refuses, she is not nashiz. She said: 'I will not live with your slave girl, and I want a separate house.' She does not have that. He (the author) said: (Her prevention) is an additive phrase, a subject, meaning the woman preventing her husband (from entering upon her) while he (i.e., the husband) lives with her in her house, (it is) nushuz, because she confined herself from him without right, so maintenance is not obligatory for her as long as she continues to prevent him, thus nushuz is realized from her, and it becomes like her confining herself in someone else's house. This applies if she prevented him intending to live in her own house. But if the prevention was to move him to his house, she is not nashiz, because residence is obligatory for her upon him, so her confining herself from him is with right, so her maintenance does not fall, because the shortcoming came from his side, so it becomes like when she confined herself to collect her dowry, unlike if she confined herself due to a debt upon her or a usurper usurped her and took her away, because the loss is not from his side, and unlike if she was living with him in his house and did not enable him for intercourse, because he can force intercourse often. He (the author) said: (And if he lives in a usurped house and she refuses, she is not nashiz) because she is right, because living in it is haram. He (the author) said: (She said: 'I will not live with your slave girl and I want a separate house' and she does not have that) because he must have someone to serve him, so he cannot be prevented from that.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rulings of nushuz (wifely disobedience). The base text states that if the wife prevents her husband from entering her house while he lives with her there, she is nashiz (disobedient). The commentator explains that because she unjustly withholds herself, she loses her right to maintenance. However, if she prevents him from moving into her house because she wants to stay in her own house (not to deny him entry altogether), she is not nashiz; similarly, if she prevents him to enforce her right to separate residence in his house, it is not nushuz. Then an exception: if the husband lives in a usurped house and she refuses to live there, she is not nashiz because she is right to avoid haram. Finally, if the wife demands a separate dwelling from the husband's slave girl, she has no right to that because the husband needs his servant. These rulings are based on the principle that nushuz forfeits maintenance, but legitimate excuses protect her right.

Key Terms
نشوز (nushūz): Wife's disobedience to her husband by withholding herself without legitimate cause.
ناشزة (nāshizah): A wife who is guilty of nushuz.
غصب (ghaṣb): Usurpation; taking property without right. Living in a usurped house is haram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because she is denying him entry to her house.
B) No, because living in a usurped house is haram and she is justified.
C) Yes, but only if she also demands separate residence.
D) No, because she has the right to choose any house.

Key Takeaway
Nushuz requires unjustified refusal; a wife is excused if the husband lives in a usurped house or if she enforces her right to separate residence, but she cannot demand a separate house from his slave girl.

The Ruling on Disputed Timing of a Gift of Mahr
Ruling on whose word is taken in dispute over mahr gift timing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whose word is taken when a wife gifts her mahr during illness and then dies, and explain the basis of istihsan over qiyas in this case.

Core Idea
The husband's word is accepted because the mahr is already dropped regardless of timing.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with a specific dispute: a wife gives her mahr to her husband as a gift, then dies. Her heirs claim the gift occurred during her death-illness (marad al-mawt), which would subject it to inheritance rules, while the husband claims it was during health. The Hanafi school resolves this by applying istihsan (juristic preference) over qiyas (analogy), accepting the husband's word and dropping the mahr. Understanding this ruling clarifies how Hanafi jurists prioritize practical outcomes over strict analogical reasoning in family law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute and parties
Recognize the conflicting claims: the heirs say the gift was during death-illness, the husband says it was during health. The legal question is whose word is accepted.
2
State the qiyas (analogy) that would favor the heirs
By qiyas, the word should be with the heirs because the gift is a recent event, and events are attributed to the nearest time (al-ḥawādith tuḍāfu ilā aqrab al-awqāt).
This is a general principle in evidence law.
3
Explain the istihsan that overrides the qiyas
The istihsan reasoning is that all parties agree (ittifāq) that the mahr is dropped from the husband regardless of the timing of the gift, because a gift during death-illness to an heir still gives full ownership. Therefore, settling the dispute on the husband's word avoids unnecessary litigation.
Istihsan is a departure from qiyas for a stronger reason, here based on the agreed outcome.
4
Understand the legal effect
The mahr is dropped from the husband, and the heirs have no right to claim it. The husband's statement suffices to resolve the dispute.
5
Note the consensus (ittifaq) on the dropping of mahr
The text highlights that there is agreement that the gift during death-illness is valid and the mahr is dropped, which forms the basis of the istihsan.
This consensus removes the need for further inquiry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهبت مهرها لزوجها فماتت فطالب ورثتها بمهرها وقالوا كانت الهبة في مرض موتها وقال بل في الصحة فالقول له أي للزوج والقياس أن يكون القول للورثة لأن الهبة حادثة والحوادث تضاف إلى أقرب الأوقات. ووجه الاستحسان اتفقوا على سقوط المهر عن الزوج لأن الهبة في مرض الموت تفيد الملك وإن كانت للوارث ألا ترى أن المريض إذا وهب لوارثه يملك الموهوب.
She donated her mahr to her husband, then she died, so her heirs demanded her mahr and said: 'The gift was during her death-illness.' And he said: 'Rather, during health.' So the word is his, i.e., the husband's. By analogy, the word should be for the heirs, because the gift is an occurrence and occurrences are attributed to the nearest times. And the istihsan reasoning is that they have agreed that the mahr is dropped from the husband, because a gift during death-illness gives ownership even if to an heir. Do you not see that if a sick person gives a gift to his heir, the donee owns the gift.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage deals with a dispute between a husband and his deceased wife's heirs. The wife donated her mahr (dowry) to her husband, then died. The heirs claim the donation occurred during her death-illness (so the gift is effective but may be subject to inheritance rules), while the husband says it was during health. By Qiyas (analogy), the word should be with the heirs because the gift is a recent event. However, Istihsan (juristic preference) dictates that the husband's word is accepted, because there is consensus that the mahr is dropped from the husband regardless, because a gift during death-illness to an heir gives full ownership. Since the matter is settled, the husband's claim is given preference to avoid unnecessary litigation.

Key Terms
مرض الموت (marad al-mawt): A terminal illness that leads to death, during which gifts are treated specially.
الاستحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference, a method of departing from analogy for a stronger reason.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the dispute over the timing of the wife's gift of mahr to her husband, whose word is taken and why?
A) The heirs' word, because the gift is a recent event.
B) The husband's word, because there is consensus that the mahr is dropped regardless.
C) The qadi decides based on evidence.
D) The wife's word before death.

Key Takeaway
In disputes over the timing of a gift during illness, Hanafi jurisprudence applies istihsan to avoid unnecessary litigation, accepting the husband's word because the mahr is already dropped.

Husband's Word Accepted on Timing of Wife's Mahr Gift
Ruling on husband's word being taken regarding timing of mahr gift

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whose word is accepted when a wife gifts her mahr and dies, and explain why istihsan overrides qiyas in this case.

Core Idea
Husband's word taken on gift timing; istihsan overrides qiyas due to consensus that mahr drops.

In this passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, we examine a dispute between a husband and his wife's heirs. The wife had gifted her mahr to her husband and then died. The heirs claimed the gift was made during her death-illness, while the husband said it was during health. The Hanafi school rules in favor of the husband, relying on istihsan despite the qiyas that would favor the heirs. Understanding this ruling reveals how juristic preference can override strict analogy when there is a stronger consideration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and claims
The wife's heirs claim the gift of mahr was made during her death-illness (marad al-mawt). The husband claims it was during health.
The dispute is about the timing of the gift, which affects its legal validity under rules of death-illness gifts.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The husband's word is accepted (al-qawl li al-zawj). This means he does not have to prove his claim; his statement is taken as true.
This is the specific ruling from the text.
3
Recognize the qiyas argument
By qiyas (analogy), the heirs' word should be accepted because the gift is a recent event, and events are normally attributed to the nearest time (i.e., the time of death).
Qiyas is based on the principle that the cause of an event is the closest possible time.
4
Understand the istihsan basis
The Hanafi school uses istihsan (juristic preference) to override the qiyas. The basis is a consensus (ittifaq) among scholars that the mahr drops from the husband, implying the gift is valid even if made in death-illness.
The consensus is that the husband does not have to pay the mahr again; this indicates the gift is effective.
5
Apply the principle
Because of this consensus, the husband's claim is accepted, and he keeps the mahr. The dispute is resolved in his favor.
This ruling avoids contradicting the established consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهبت مهرها لزوجها فماتت فطالب ورثتها بمهرها وقالوا كانت الهبة في مرض موتها وقال بل في الصحة فالقول له أي للزوج والقياس أن يكون القول للورثة لأن الهبة حادثة والحوادث تضاف إلى أقرب الأوقات. ووجه الاستحسان اتفقوا على سقوط المهر عن الزوج لأن الهبة في مرض الموت تفيد الملك وإن كانت للوارث ألا ترى أن المريض إذا
She gifted her mahr to her husband, then she died, so her heirs demanded her mahr from him and said the gift was during her death-illness, and he said it was during health; his statement is accepted (meaning the husband's). By analogy, the statement should be the heirs' because the gift is an event and events are attributed to the nearest time. And the basis of istihsan is that they agreed on the dropping of the mahr from the husband because the gift during death-illness confers ownership even if it is for the heir. Do you not see that the sick person if...
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Hanafi school. The matn states: when a wife gifts her mahr to her husband and then dies, and the heirs claim it was given during her death-illness (marad al-mawt) while the husband says it was during health, the husband's word is accepted (al-qawl lahu). The commentator explains that by qiyas (analogy), the word should go to the heirs because the gift is a recent event and events are attributed to the closest time. However, istihsan (juristic preference) overrides this analogy because there is a consensus (ittifaq) among the scholars that the mahr drops from the husband. The reason: a gift during death-illness confers ownership even if it is to an heir, but since they agreed on dropping the mahr, it indicates the gift is considered valid, and the husband's claim is accepted. The text cuts off but the principle is clear.

Key Terms
هبة المهر (hibat al-mahr): Gift of the dower (mahr) by the wife to her husband
مرض الموت (marad al-mawt): Death-illness: a sickness from which one is expected to die and actually dies
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas due to a stronger consideration

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a wife gifts her mahr to her husband and then dies, and her heirs claim it was in death-illness while the husband claims it was in health, whose word is accepted according to the Hanafi school?
A) Heirs, because events are attributed to the nearest time.
B) The husband, due to istihsan and consensus.
C) Both must provide evidence.
D) The witnesses' testimony decides.

Key Takeaway
The husband's word is accepted on the timing of his wife's mahr gift due to istihsan based on consensus that the mahr drops.
