Wife's Gift of Mahr Under Threat of Beating
Ruling on validity of gift when wife fears beating

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a gift of mahr given under threat of beating and explain why a credible threat invalidates the gift.

Core Idea
A gift of mahr under credible threat of beating is invalid because coercion negates mutual consent.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq expands on a ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It addresses a specific marital scenario: a husband threatens his wife with beating, and she gives him her mahr as a gift out of fear. The ruling hinges on the husband's ability to carry out the threat, which establishes coercion in property matters. Understanding this principle is key to distinguishing valid gifts from those invalidated by duress.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a wife gifting her mahr to her husband. The ruling is that the gift is not valid (lam yasihh) if the husband is able to beat her.
The ruling applies only when the threat is credible—i.e., the husband can actually carry it out.
2
Determine the condition for invalidity
The condition is that the husband is capable of beating her (qadara 'ala al-darb). This makes the threat credible, establishing coercion (ikrah).
If the husband is not able to beat her (e.g., physically unable or restrained), the threat may not be credible.
3
Understand the legal basis
Coercion in property matters (ikrah 'ala al-mal) is established by a credible threat of harm. Mutual consent (taradi) is a condition for transferring ownership, and consent is negated by such threats.
The Hanafi principle: any transaction made under duress that negates free consent is void (batil).
4
Apply the ruling to the scenario
Since the wife gifted the mahr under fear of beating, and the husband could beat her, the gift is invalid. The mahr remains the wife's property.
If the threat were not credible, the gift might be valid—but that requires separate analysis.
5
Note cross-madhhab differences
Some schools may require actual coercion (e.g., beating occurred) to invalidate; Hanafis consider a credible threat sufficient.
This ikhtilaf affects how strictly one interprets 'fear' in property transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (خوفها بالضرب حتى وهبته مهرها لم يصح أن قدر على الضرب لأنها مكرهة عليه إذا لإكراه على المال يثبت بمثله لأن التراضي شرط في تمليك الأموال والرضا ينتفي بمثله فلا قال يصح .
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (If he frightens her with beating until she gives him her mahr as a gift, it is not valid if he is able to beat her, because she is coerced. Because coercion regarding property is established by such means, since mutual consent is a condition for transferring ownership of property, and consent is negated by such means, so it is not valid.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the matn expanded by the commentator. It addresses the scenario where a husband threatens his wife with beating, and out of fear she gives him her mahr (dower) as a gift. If the husband is capable of carrying out the beating (i.e., the threat is credible), then the gift is invalid. The reason is that coercion in property matters is established by such threats, because mutual consent (taradi) is a condition for the transfer of property ownership. In Hanafi law, consent is negated by credible threats of physical harm. Therefore, the gift is not valid.

Key Terms
مهر (mahr): The dower or bridal gift given by the husband to the wife as part of marriage.
إكراه على المال (ikrah 'ala al-mal): Coercion that affects property transactions, invalidating them if it negates free consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband threatens to beat his wife and she gives him her mahr, when is the gift invalid?
A) Only if the beating actually occurs
B) If the husband is able to beat her (credible threat)
C) Only if the wife testifies in court
D) The gift is always valid regardless of threat

Key Takeaway
A gift of mahr made under credible threat of beating is batil because coercion negates the essential condition of mutual consent in property transfers.

Building on Wife's Property: Permission and Ownership
Rulings on ownership of improvements on wife's property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on ownership and debt when a husband builds on his wife's property in three scenarios: with permission, without permission for himself, and without permission for her.

Core Idea
Ownership of building follows intent; permission creates a debt, not a gift.

This lesson examines the juridical status of improvements made by a husband to his wife's property. The key factor is the wife's permission and the husband's intention. The rulings demonstrate how ownership and financial obligations are determined in the marital home context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
The passage presents three distinct scenarios: (1) with permission, (2) without permission but building for himself, (3) without permission but building for her.
The differing outcomes hinge on permission and intent.
2
Analyze the first scenario: with permission
When the husband builds with his wife's permission, the building becomes her property. The expenses he incurred become a debt owed by the wife to the husband.
This is because he acted as her agent or with her consent; the building attaches to her land.
3
Analyze the second scenario: without permission, for himself
If the husband builds on her property without permission but with the intention that the building be his own (e.g., he uses his own materials and treats it as his), the building remains his property. He may remove it if the wife demands.
This is based on the principle that one does not lose ownership of his property without consent.
4
Analyze the third scenario: without permission, for her
If he builds without her permission but with the intention to benefit her, the building becomes her property. He is considered a volunteer (mutatawwi') and cannot claim reimbursement.
He acted gratuitously; the law presumes he intended a gift.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ولو عمر دار زوجته بماله بإذنها فالعمارة لها والنفقة دين عليها لأن الملك لها) وقد صح (بلا إذنها فله) ولو عمرها لها بلا إذنها فالعمارة لها وهو متطوع ولو أخذ غريمة فنزعه انسان من يده لم يضمن (في يده مال انسان فقال له سلطان ادفع إلى هذا المال وإلا اقطع يدك أو أضربك خمسين فدفع لم يضمن) أي لا يضمن الدافع لأنه مكره عليه فكان الضمان على المكره أو على الآخذ أيهما شاء المالك إذا كان الآخذ مختاراً وإلا فعلى المكره فقط .
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And if he builds his wife's house with his wealth with her permission, then the building is hers and the expenses are a debt upon her) because the ownership is hers. And (without her permission, then it is his). And if he builds it for her without her permission, then the building is hers and he is a volunteer (mutatawwi').
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage is a set of rulings from the matn expanded by the commentator. It addresses three scenarios when a husband builds on his wife's property: (1) With her permission – the building becomes her property, and he is entitled to reimbursement of expenses as a debt on her. (2) Without her permission but building for himself – the building is his property (the materials remain his). (3) Without her permission but building for her – the building becomes her property, and he is considered a volunteer (mutatawwi'), meaning he cannot claim reimbursement because he did so without her request. The commentator later discusses if he builds for himself without permission: the building is his, but he is trespassing on her land and must remove it if she demands.

Key Terms
متطوع (mutatawwi'): A volunteer; one who does an act without expecting compensation. In this context, the husband who builds on his wife's property without her permission intending it for her has no right to reimbursement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband builds on his wife's property with her permission, what is the ruling regarding ownership and the husband's financial claim?
A) The building belongs to the husband, and he has no claim
B) The building belongs to the wife, and the husband has a debt claim
C) The building is jointly owned
D) The husband is a volunteer and cannot claim expenses

Key Takeaway
Ownership of improvements on a spouse's property hinges on permission and intent: with permission, she owns and he has a debt; without permission, his intent determines ownership or volunteerism.

Forced Payment of Another's Debt Under Duress
Ruling on liability for forced payment of another's debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when a person is forced to hand over another's property to a third party under threat, and identify who is liable.

Core Idea
The coerced payer is not liable; liability falls on the coercer or the recipient if the recipient is a free agent.

This lesson addresses the legal consequences of paying a debt on behalf of another under duress. The Hanafi school distinguishes between the coerced person (who is excused) and the coercer (who may be liable). This ruling protects vulnerable individuals from being forced into financial loss.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
A person (A) has in his hand property belonging to another (B). The ruler (sultan) says to A: 'Pay this money to someone (C) or else I will cut off your hand or strike you fifty lashes.' A pays under duress.
The question is: who is liable for the loss to B?
2
Apply the ruling: payer not liable
The passage states: 'he is not liable' – meaning A (the payer) does not guarantee the property because he was coerced.
Coercion (ikrah) removes the element of free choice, hence no liability.
3
Determine liability based on recipient's status
If the recipient (C) is a free agent (i.e., not coerced), then the owner (B) may choose to claim from either the coercer (the ruler) or the recipient (C). But if C is also coerced, then liability falls only on the coercer.
This is because the actual taking was by C, but if C was coerced, he is like a tool.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ولو عمر دار زوجته بماله بإذنها فالعمارة لها والنفقة دين عليها لأن الملك لها) وقد صح (بلا إذنها فله) ولو عمرها لها بلا إذنها فالعمارة لها وهو متطوع ولو أخذ غريمة فنزعه انسان من يده لم يضمن (في يده مال انسان فقال له سلطان ادفع إلى هذا المال وإلا اقطع يدك أو أضربك خمسين فدفع لم يضمن) أي لا يضمن الدافع لأنه مكره عليه فكان الضمان على المكره أو على الآخذ أيهما شاء المالك إذا كان الآخذ مختاراً وإلا فعلى المكره فقط .
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And if he builds his wife's house with his wealth with her permission, then the building is hers and the expenses are a debt upon her) because the ownership is hers. And (without her permission, then it is his). And if he builds it for her without her permission, then the building is hers and he is a volunteer (mutatawwi').
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage is a set of rulings from the matn expanded by the commentator. It addresses three scenarios when a husband builds on his wife's property: (1) With her permission – the building becomes her property, and he is entitled to reimbursement of expenses as a debt on her. (2) Without her permission but building for himself – the building is his property (the materials remain his). (3) Without her permission but building for her – the building becomes her property, and he is considered a volunteer (mutatawwi'), meaning he cannot claim reimbursement because he did so without her request. The commentator later discusses if he builds for himself without permission: the building is his, but he is trespassing on her land and must remove it if she demands.

Key Terms
مكره (mukrah): Coerced person; one who is forced to act under threat of harm. In this context, the payer who is forced to hand over another's property is not liable.
ضمان (daman): Liability or guarantee. The text discusses on whom the guarantee for the lost property falls.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) The recipient only
D) The owner may choose between the coercer and the recipient

Key Takeaway
A coerced payer is not liable; the owner may claim from the coercer or from a free-agent recipient.

Building on Wife's Land: Ownership and Liability
Rulings on construction on wife's land with and without permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for construction on a wife's land with and without permission and identify whether the husband is a usurper or a volunteer.

Core Idea
Building on wife's land with own materials: husband owns construction but must vacate on demand; building for her without permission gives her ownership and no claim.

This lesson examines two scenarios from the Hanafi school regarding a husband building on his wife's property. The key distinction is whether the husband builds for himself or for her, and whether he has permission. Understanding these rulings clarifies the boundaries of ownership and liability in spousal property dealings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two cases: building for himself without her permission, and building for her without her permission. Note the difference in intention.
Focus on the phrases 'for himself' and 'for her'.
2
Determine ownership of materials
In both cases, the husband uses his own building materials (the 'tool'). Ownership of materials remains with him unless he gives them as a gift.
Hanafi principle: property does not leave ownership without owner's consent.
3
Apply the legal status
If he builds for himself without permission, he is a usurper (ghāṣib) of the land. If he builds for her without permission, he is a volunteer (mutaṭawwi‘). These statuses determine his rights and obligations.
A ghāṣib must vacate on demand; a mutaṭawwi‘ cannot claim reimbursement.
4
State the remedies
In scenario 1, the wife can demand removal of the building. In scenario 2, the building becomes hers and the husband has no claim for costs.
The remedy flows from the legal status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنفسه بلا إذنها فله أي إذا عمر لنفسه من غير إذن المرأة كانت العمارة له لأن الآلة التي بنى بها ملكه فلا يخرج عن ملكه بالبناء من غير رضاه فيبقى على ملكه ويكون غاضباً للعرصة وشاغلاً ملك غیره بملكه فيؤمر بالتفريغ إن طلبت زوجته ذلك . ولو عمرها لها بلا إذنها فالعمارة لها وهو متطوع أي عمرها لها بغير إذنها كان لها البناء وهو متطوع بالبناء فلا يكون له الرجوع عليها به لأنه لا ولاية له في ايجاب ذلك عليها
And for himself without her permission, it is his. That is, if he builds for himself without the wife's permission, the construction is his because the tools with which he built are his property, so they do not leave his ownership by building without his consent, so they remain his property, and he is a usurper of the land and occupies another's property with his own, so he is ordered to vacate if his wife demands that. And if he builds for her without her permission, the construction is hers, and he is a volunteer. That is, he built for her without her permission; the building is hers, and he is a volunteer in the building, so he cannot reclaim from her because he has no authority to impose that upon her.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings from the Hanafi school regarding construction on a wife's land. The type is a ruling statement with explanation. First, if a husband builds on his wife's land using his own materials without her permission, the building remains his property. However, he is considered a usurper of the land (ghasib) and must remove his building if the wife demands. Second, if the husband builds on her land intending the building to be for her without her permission, the building becomes her property and he is considered a volunteer (mutatawwi‘), meaning he cannot claim reimbursement because he had no authority to obligate her. The reasoning is based on ownership of the materials and lack of authority.

Key Terms
غاصب (ghāṣib): One who wrongfully occupies or uses another's property without permission.
متطوع (mutaṭawwi‘): One who performs an act voluntarily without expectation of compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband builds on his wife's land using his own materials without her permission, who owns the building according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) The wife owns the building because it is on her land.
B) The husband owns the building but must vacate if the wife demands.
C) The building belongs to the state.
D) The building is jointly owned.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, building on another's land without permission does not transfer ownership of the building; the builder retains ownership but may be required to remove it or be considered a volunteer.
